1442359919|5.0|Devon Jones', rising college Sophomore, gets a job in a small, quaint amusement park on the coast of South Carolina, he did so to earn money for college and have some place to be while his girlfriend became a \\"Filene's Girl\\" in Boston for the summer. When said girl breaks his heart, he is lucky to be a member of the \\"Joyland\\" family, where he could \\"wait until she comes back\\" and nurse his pain during the wait.  His work ethic and unique physical appearance (redheaded and stands 6'4\\") gets him quickly noticed in the park, his work \\"wearing the fur\\" (donning the costume of \\"Happy Hound,\\" the park's mascot, in the South Carolina summer sun) distinguishes him from the other \\"summer greenies\\" (the designation all new employees are given). Within his first hours of employment, he learns of the unsolved murder in the \\"park's only dark  ride\\" (Horror House) four years previous, of a young lady whose ghost is reported to haunt the ride and occasionally show herself to park employees, always \\"after the park closes.\\"<br />Mr. King takes this possibly pedestrian plot and creates a memorable fun, delightfully thrilling, \\"old school\\" crime novel set in 1973.  Told from the first person perspective of Devon \\"Dev\\" Jones, the reader is given the occasion to remember/experience a time when gas was $11 a barrel, summer break lasted until after Labor Day and murders (seemed to be) a rare occurrence.  It is the innocence of this age that makes the plot both plausible and frightening; the methods by which crimes were solved were much more primitive and only in a time when \\"CSI\\" investigative techniques are not common knowledge could the murderer elude capture for so long.<br />This is a \\"Coming-of-Age\\" story as well.  Devon's experience of being on his own in a culture foreign to him gives a backdrop to the crime allowing the reader to experience of being \\"on the outside\\" as well.  Devon (and the reader) must learn the language, traditions and taboos of the Amusement Park/\\"Carny\\" society as he becomes a member of that family.  Mr. King uses this process as a powerful metaphor for Devon's growing into an adulthood where the language sounds the same but the words come with much more responsibility, traditions are created for oneself and old traditions are renewed or discarded consistent with the life one is discovering and those evils from which a child is sheltered are exposed in all their Technicolor flashes.<br />This is a book for adults in the style of Mickey Spillane and Dashiell Hammett crime novels.  Violence exists but only (occasionally graphically) glimpsed, sex is a given, but only in whispered suggestions, the crimes are gruesome & random and the criminal loathsome.  This volume is the second in a series from the \\"Hard Case Crime\\" publishers and it is good enough that I want to read the first installment.

0316017930|4.0|The main premise of the book, as outlined by Malcom is: \\"In Outliers, I want to convince you that these kinds of personal explanations of success don't work. People don't rise from nothing. We do owe something to parentage and patronage. The people who stand before kings may look like they did it all by themselves. But in fact they are invariably the beneficiaries of hidden advantages and extraordinary opportunities and cultural legacies that allow them to learn and work hard and make sense of the world in ways others cannot. It makes a difference where and when we grew up. The culture we belong to and the legacies passed down by our forebears shape the patterns of our achievement in ways we cannot begin to imagine. It's not enough to ask what successful people are like, in other words. It is mainly by asking where they dire from that we can unravel the logic behind who succeeds and who doesn't.\\"<br /><br />Below are key excerpts from the book that I found particularly insightful:<br /><br />1- \\"Wolf and Bruhn had to convince the medical establishment to think about health and heart attacks in an entirely new way: they had to get them to realize that they wouldn't be able to understand why someone was healthy if all they did was think about an individual's personal choices or actions in isolation. They had to look beyond the individual. They had to understand the culture he or she was a part of, and who their friends and families were, and what town their families came from. They had to appreciate the idea that the values of the world we inhabit and the people we surround ourselves with have a profound effect on who we are. In Outliers I want to do for our understanding of success what Stewart Wolf did for our understanding of health.\\"<br /><br />2- \\"Their research suggests that once a musician has enough ability to get into a top music school, the thing that distinguishes one performer from another is how hard he or she works. That's it. And what's more, the people at the very top don't work just harder or even much harder than everyone else. They work much, much harder.\\"<br /><br />3- \\"We pretend that success is exclusively a matter of individual merit. But there's nothing in any of the histories we've looked at so far to suggest things are that simple. These are stories, instead, about people who were given a special opportunity to work really hard and seized it, and who happened to come of age at a time when that extraordinary effort was rewarded by the rest of society. Their success was not just of their own making. It was a product of the world in which they grew up.\\"<br /><br />4- \\"The relationship between success and IQ works only up to a point. Once someone has reached an IQ of somewhere around 120, having additional IQ points doesn't seem to translate into any measurable real-world advantage.\\"<br /><br />5- \\"Those three things--autonomy, complexity, and a connection between effort and reward--are, most people agree, the three qualities that work has to have if it is to be satisfying...Work that fulfills those three criteria is meaningful.\\"<br /><br />6- \\"So far in Outliers we've seen that success arises out of the steady accumulation of advantages: when and where you are born, what your parents did tor a living, and what the circumstances of your upbringing were all make a significant difference in how well you do in the world. The question for the second part of Outliers is whether the traditions and attitudes we inherit from our forebears can play the same role. Can we learn something about why people succeed and how to make people better at what they do by taking cultural legacies seriously? 1 think we can.\\"<br /><br />7- \\"Each of us has his or her own distinct personality. But overlaid on top of that are tendencies and assumptions and reflexes handed down to us by the history of the community we grew up in, and those differences are extraordinarily specific.\\"<br /><br />8- \\"The lesson here is very simple. But itis striking how ten it is overlooked. We are so caught in the myths ot the best and the brightest and the self-made that we think outliers spring naturally from the earth. We look at the young Bill Gates and marvel that our world allowed that thirteen-year-old to become a fabulously successful entrepreneur. But that's the wrong lesson. Our world only allowed one thirteen-year-old unlimited access to a timesharing terminal in 1968. If a million teenagers had been given the same opportunity, how many more Microsofts would we have today? To build a better world we need to replace the patchwork of lucky breaks and arbitrary advantages that today determine success--the fortunate birth dates and the happy accidents of history--with a society that provides opportunities for all.\\"<br /><br />9- \\"Superstar lawyers and math whizzes and software entrepreneurs appear at first blush to lie outside ordinary experience. But they don't. They are products of history and community, of opportunity and legacy. Their success is not exceptional or mysterious. It is grounded in a web of advantages and inheritances, some deserved, some not, some earned, some just plain lucky--but all critical to making them who they are. The outlier, in the end, is not an outlier at all.\\"is striking how ten it is overlooked. 1451626096|1.0|This review is about the book The White Princess by Phillippa Gregory, published July 23, 2013, fourth in the Cousins' War Series, soon to be a series by BBC, available in hardback, paperback, audiobook, and Kindle edition, 544 pages long, ISBN number...<br /><br />  I am giving this book one star not just because I am unable to give it no stars, but because it is bloody hard to write like that.  Phillipa Gregory deserves kudos for catering to the evidently large percentage of her readers who suffer from otherwise debilitating medical conditions.  The following is a list of those disorders whose sufferers this book is obviously directed towards.<br /><br />*Recurring Retrograde Amnesia*: The frustrations of forgetting what you read ten pages ago are a thing of the past.  Not only that, but you will no longer be frustrated by forgetting what you read ten pages ago!  Who is this book about? Elizabeth of York.  Who does she love? Her uncle, Richard III.  What house is she from?  The Royal House of York!  (Ten pages later...)  Who is this book about?...<br />  *Non-sufferers: For those who do not suffer from RRA, there will be an exam on this later.<br /><br />*Dysfunctional Palate*:  Finally! A book that respects the ability to stomach ANYTHING.  Ms. Gregory understands the sideways glances you normally receive because you don't gag over a young girl recounting her romantic trysts with her deceased uncle.  Normal readers will be envious of the ease with which you swallow Elizabeth's tearful recollections of her plans with her lover, Uncle Richard, & the children they would have had, the kingdom they would have run, the steamy nights they would have enjoyed, ad nauseaum (but not for you!)<br />  *Non-sufferers: Though gag reflex-invoking and complete fabrication, an uncle-niece union was not unheard of in royal marriages.  The House of Hapsburg inbred itself to extinction with familial interrelations.  In the present day, many families have a \\"funny uncle,\\" and if there was a war going on, he would likely be abandoned, stabbed, and left for dead.  Like Richard III.<br /><br />*Fantasy-prone Personality*: Two family lines battle over a crown in a war that lasts over 30 years, ravages a country still marred by the 100 Years War, & ripples through history to affect the royal dynasty to this day.  There are plots and schemes, murders, sword fights, betrayals, greedy ambitions, and trials of the heart.<br />  How mundane.<br />  Because history is so very dull, Ms. Gregory has spiced it up to tantalize the senses of even the most colourful and abstract dreamers.  You will enjoy witchcraft (including a relatively non-specific curse,) a hunt for a murderer made much more enjoyable by the complete disregard of facts from heavily researched events, a rape to begin painting what would ordinarily have been a decent king with a tainted brush, and a pretender to Prince Richard who actually isn't pretending!  Ms. Gregory, with a touch of artistic license, manages to embellish historical events so well even those with FPP will be unable to resist.  Finally, FPP sufferers can be part of the real (um...) world.<br />  *Non-sufferers: Even I had to admire the pressing of poetically-licensed boundaries.  King Henry VII, at his mother's behest, raped his future bride while everyone else was having dinner in the next room.  I NEVER could have dreamed something like that up.  I do think Ms. Gregory did the readers a grave injustice by not including the conversation between his mother (Margaret Beaufort, Countess of Richmond and Derby, My Lady the King's Mother...) & Henry that led up to this decision.  \\"Henry, you've outgrown that shirt. Oh and right before dessert...\\"<br /><br />*Blunted Affect*:  This disorder is perhaps the most obviously & prevalently catered to by this book.  It would otherwise be impossible to read it in any sort of company.  Your skills of stoicism will finally be challenged when you read Elizabeth's pining without disgust, herrape without horror, her growing love for her rapist without stupefaction, \\"the Royal House of York\\" line without irritation, & the rest without zeal because the end MUST be in sight.<br />  *Non-sufferers: Read this book in private or you will be beleaguered by questions asking if you are okay and do you need a bin in case you are sick.<br /><br />  Unfortunately, I do not suffer from any of the aforementioned ailments.  Consequently, this book was difficult to read and enjoy with its unnecessary elaborations on already interesting historical events, repetitive dialogue in unrealistic human interactions, and solicitations of strong, negative emotions to the point I felt nauseous.  I typically enjoy Ms. Gregory's books, but this book was so far from her baseline style of writing for this era, I felt as if it was certainly not written for the typical audience.  Sufferers of above-mentioned disorders are my most educated guess as to whom Ms. Gregory was writing for, because it certainly was not for me.  -C.rape without horror, her growing love for her rapist without stupefaction, \\"the Royal House of York\\" line without irritation, & the rest without zeal because the end MUST be in sight.<br />  *Non-sufferers: Read this book in private or you will be beleaguered by questions asking if you are okay and do you need a bin in case you are sick.<br /><br />  Unfortunately, I do not suffer from any of the aforementioned ailments. But the Master pales in comparison to the greatest threat of all - the return of the Time Lords!  This is the best regeneration story I've seen, apart from possibly the Caves of Androzani.  A couple nitpicks though.  The Doctor's tantrum at having to die when he struggles to decide whether to save Wilf or not was out of character for me, & as usual I hated the bit with Captain Jack, but apart from those 2 scenes this was perfect.  The story was interesting & epic.  We learn why the Master has been crazy all this time & we get a good end to his story.  The scenes of the Doctor visiting his companions were all wonderful, except for the aforementioned scene with Captain Jack.  The Ood singing the Doctor to sleep was one of the saddest, most beautiful things I've ever seen on television.  And of course to top it all off the Eleventh Doctor gets a great entrance.  All in all this is really good.  A+<br /><br />Farewell David Tennant.  You were truly the greatest Doctor.  Matt Smith is doing his part in following in your footsteps; it's just that he needs better stories to go along with his great acting.  Here's hoping Series 7 gets back to the quality of the first 5 series after Amy & Rory leave.

0374203032|4.0|In this book, popular Harvard professor Michael Sandel addresses the question of when it is inappropriate to use market solutions to social problems. Twenty years ago, such a book might have seemed nonsensical, but in recent years, economists promoting markets have gained prominence in many fields -- pay kids to do their homework, pay poor people not to have babies, etc. And in areas far from public policy, explicit markets have thrived -- pay homeless people to wait in line for you, pay kids to gather autographs from baseball players, etc.  Though many economists celebrate such developments as beneficial for all and harmful for none, Sandel is not so sure.<br /><br />Much of the book amounts to catalogs of examples like these. This portion of the book is fun to read, but lacks depth and is never very convincing. \\"Here's an example that surprised me. Here's an amusing quotation from a New York Times article about it. Economists think it's great, but I'm not so sure. Next example!\\" Three chapters deal with particular markets in detail (the market for spots in line, the market for life insurance, and \\"naming rights\\" in stadiums and schools, etc.), and are worthy of comment. The chapter on insurance seemed in many regards naive. Sandel points out that some whose policies were sold dislike it, but unless we take the view that every participant in a transaction should be happy afterwards, I don't see where this is an ethical issue. He then points out that some brokers of the policies misbehaved and broke laws. True, but the same can probably be said for automobile dealers and supermarkets; do we really want to eliminate markets for cars or food? More seriously, Sandel just doesn't seem to understand insurance very well. He nowhere discusses the fact that, absent a secondary market, life insurance carriers routinely return only the cash surrender value of a policy to an insured who needs to get out early. That's the equivalent of your selling a car and getting only the cash back from the glove compartment. Only the secondary market pays the insured for the actuarial value of the policy. Would Sandel argue that it's ethically better to allow life insurance companies to steal from their customers? Probably not, but he manages to devote about 20% of the book to this subject and I don't think he really understands it.<br /><br />The chapter on naming rights is similarly unconvincing. Who cares if sports teams have logos on their jerseys? I appreciate it was different when Sandel was a kid and watched the the Twins play for $2, but much of this chapter comes across as [[ASIN:1586487736 Andy Rooney]]ish-grumbling by an old man. The portion of the chapter about why we shouldn't let companies advertise in schools is better, but also sort of obvious.<br /><br />So why four stars rather than fewer? The book does make an important, convincing argument in chapter 3. In fact, this chapter is good enough that I suggest readers start there, and perhaps skip the rest of the book. In that chapter, Sandel argues that we all benefit from civic virtues, but that civic virtues can be \\"crowded out\\" by market forces and are hard to restore. For example, students who raise money for charity because it's the \\"right thing\\" to do will raise more money than those who are paid a commission, but once you have introduced the idea of a commission, all the students will work less hard at their fundraising, because the social-spiritedness of the enterprise has been tainted. Sandel provides numerous examples, all of them excellent.sh back from the glove compartment. Only the secondary market pays the insured for the actuarial value of the policy. Probably not, but he manages to devote about 20% of the book to this subject and I don't think he really understands it.<br /><br />The chapter on naming rights is similarly unconvincing. B00AFPVPDG|5.0|I had written and posted a long-winded review of this book, but less is always more. My main points are this. FWIW, I have worked with Josh and vouch strongly for the depth and accuracy of his research. Ignore the negative reviews here unless one says specifically that the reviewer read the book, actually tried the strategies JK promotes and then articulates why they did not work. Notice - none of them do that.<br /><br />If ever there was a writer who was \\"deceptively simple\\" it is Josh. He has a gift for distilling things down to their most essential qualities and for putting them in terms that are practical and easy to execute in real life.<br /><br />The bottom line is the book does exactly what it claims - teaches you skills to get up to speed on things you want to do as quickly as possible and with minimal stress. I read it, am using it, and in my experience it works. But most importantly if learning new skills tends to stress you out then going through each chapter, and seeing how someone with Josh's unique level of intelligent, laid back determination solves problems and acquires skills rapidly, will be helpful. Definitely ignore the reviews that claim the skills Josh learned are not helpful unless you want to learn those specific skills. It's the mindset toward solving different kinds of problems that is key.nsitions and title cards then export the video into a format we can post on our site. #2 says \\"Focus your energy on one skill at a time.\\" Okay, easy enough - import the video up first, etc.  In Chapter Three, \\"Ten Principles of Effective Learning\\" the first principle is \\"Research the Skill and Related Topics.\\" For me this meant re-upping my subscription to Lynda.Com, ($25.00 per month, highly recommended) and reviewing the related training for A.P.E.<br /><br />Are some of these principles obvious? Yes. Does it make things simple? Yes. But busy people with full-time jobs, families to support and long lists of things to learn need simplicity. These are big pluses. I honestly think some of the negative reviewers would have liked this book much better had the author larded it with 100-page chapters filled with charts, graphs, stats, references to Universities, etc. It's as if they would prefer a treasure map to require a DaVinci Code when all it needs is some red dots and a big \\"X.\\"<br /><br />IGNORE THE NEGATIVE REVIEWS<br />Here's the reality of this book. Josh is a researcher, teacher and writer. He is basically saying to people, \\"Hey, I have developed a systematic approach to learning new things quickly and field tested it, acquiring a passable level of skill in six very different areas. I'm concerned that people have been turned off to learning new skills because they think they have to \\"master\\" everything and don't have the time. If you give me less than $20 (just $7.50 for the paperback), I'll share my ideas with you.\\" That's it. That's the simple human being-to-human being offer being made here.<br /><br />So, for this Josh has been called, a \\"fake\\" a \\"plagiarist\\" and the book derided as \\"superficial\\" a \\"rip-off\\" and \\"poseurism.\\" Some reviewers astonishingly even claim the book doesn't offer a systematic approach. This despite the fact that chapters two and three meticulously spell out the entire system. At the risk of seeming defensive or emotional, I can't let the slander someone I know to be righteous go unchallenged. This simple, he is very well read and does not steal. But here's the bigger point about the absurdity of the negative reviews found here. Not a single one says this, \\"I tried Josh's method and it didn't work\\" or even this, \\"I tried Josh's method and it took me 21 hours.\\"<br /><br />WHY JOSH KAUFMAN IS \\"THE DUDE\\" OF INDEPENDENT LEARNING<br />In Roger Ebert's brilliant review of the classic Coen Brothers film he writes, `The Big Lebowski' is about an attitude, not a story. It's easy to miss that, because the story is so urgently pursued.\\"<br /><br />This is the real genius of this book. Beyond Rapid Skill Acquisition, it's about an attitude. It takes you into the mind of someone who is intelligent, patient and truly loves to learn.<br /><br />So, if you're thinking about buying this book, ignore the negative reviews here. Forget about Tim Ferris, Malcolm Gladwell or whether or not you want to learn the exact same skills Josh did. Forget how this book compares to Josh's classic, The Personal MBA. Just decide if you would like to develop a stress free mindset about learning new skills and a simple, effective methodology backed by high quality research. If you want to learn a new skill and are willing to give it 20 hours of focused energy, you will be amazed by how far you get. That's the simple promise made by this book and it delivers. That speaks volumes for a company's integrity and efforts to provide quality service. Please note, the mouse is NOT defective in any way, so I have upranked my review to 4 stars. The LED light is cool and the little set of weights to vary the heaviness of the base are easy to install. There's no skipping or drift that I noticed in this mouse's abilities. I would say overall it's a very solid device.<br /><br />But now for why I did not rate it higher. It's too big, seriously. Granted, I'm a female, and my hands are not huge, but they aren't tiny mouse paws either. I have long, thin piano player's fingers and I can normally reach a full octave so I should not have any problems holding this mouse. I found the palm of my hand constantly sliding down the back of the mouse and I was always aware of my thumb trying to stay put on the little indentation area below the three buttons there. They made a kind of grippy material there, but it always feels like my thumb is sweaty there, sliding down to the base and it's not sweaty, I'm just having a hard time gripping the mouse comfortably. The left and right click buttons are fine and the scroll wheel is ok, but on the other side, my pinky is also not sure where to be because I'm always trying to readjust my grip.<br /><br />Now granted, I just came from using a R.A.T. -5, a much higher end mouse (that frankly has a very crappy left and right click button that wears out too easily for the high cost (L button broke). I wasn't expecting the Anker to blow me away since I am on a budget right now. But I am disappointed it doesn't fit my hand at all and is making gaming uncomfortable for me.<br /><br />Summary: Great mouse in technical use and precision, but not recommended for those with small hands or a more claw-grip style of holding the mouse..<br /><br />Now granted, I just came from using a R.A.T. -5, a much higher end mouse (that frankly has a very crappy left and right click button that wears out too easily for the high cost (L button broke). B000FBFN1U|2.0|Since beginning this series I have gone from being excited over the material to only hopeful, then I slipped into a vague sense of annoyance, and now I'm simply disenchanted with it. I have issues with the storytelling and also the writing itself at times. I'll try to list the reasons (in no particular order) why I give Martin's work only 2 stars.<br /><br />*** Beware -- minor spoilers ahead ***<br /><br />1) Every chapter from a different POV can be interesting at times, but it gets old, because one's favorite characters are visited sporadically. And by the third book, this method also slows the story to an absolute crawl. At times it even goes backwards!<br /><br />2) Too many characters! I actually had no problem with this until this third installment. But I've finally tired of slogging through details about minor characters, their houses, their sigils, their alignments, their histories. Yes, it gives the world an epic feel, but it's too much.<br /><br />3) Many people like the \\"grey\\" characters of this series (they are not totally good or totally bad). That's fine by me. A good example here is Sandor Clegane, the Hound. He is an intriguing character. But Martin makes him and others like him so darned \\"grey\\" that they go nowhere in the story. They are so stuck in the middle that they really do nothing but create filler.<br /><br />4) Every honorable character in the story (Hi, Ned Stark) are nave morons, lacking even a smidgeon of foresight. They usually act in illogical and stupid ways, and even when they do show some smarts, they are crushed by their all-knowing adversaries. I am actually afraid to become emotionally invested in a character because I just know that Martin will run them through a medieval gauntlet of horrors, and then kill them off. I realize that characters must die. In war, there is death on both sides. But again, too much here.<br /><br />5) The age of Arya Stark. She is what, ten years old? No ten year old could be capable of doing or enduring what she does. I do like Arya, but her plot (along with all the others) is never ending and I've lost patience with it.<br /><br />6) Sex anyone? How about graphic vulgar sex? Would you like a detailed description of Tyrion's private parts? I didn't want it, but now it's etched in my mind! And another thing is Martin's fondness for nipples. In this story, every man in the world likes to pinch them. What a turn off.<br /><br />7) No plot resolution anywhere. The reader gets no closure, even on subplots. We are all running in place here.<br /><br />8) Dany Targaryen - the woman grown! I am so sick of her telling everyone that she is no child. She is thirteen years old, spends a year as Drogo's play toy, and suddenly she has worldly wisdom and is a woman grown. Please. I wanted to like her, but she is getting as bad as her brother Viserys was, thinking the whole world owes her an army.<br /><br />9) Why doesn't Westeros know about the dragons yet? Is Varys the Spider losing his mojo?<br /><br />10) Martin spends far too much time inside each character's head. He ends up doing a lot of telling versus showing. He uses flashbacks a lot. And far too often, major events happen \\"off screen\\". We read about them after the fact, by a far away character simply being told about the event. Martin managed to vanquish two armies with Melisandra, which I thought was a little lazy.<br /><br />12) Alliteration exists all over the place. Word echoes too. The author is very fond of a few words and phrases, such as Trestle table, Trencher of bread, Soldier pines, manhood,  people doing things \\"not ungently\\" or \\"not indifferently\\", Tyrion's \\"waddling\\", food grease running down chins and beards.<br /><br />Yes, I'm nitpicking. I just wanted to vent everything that irritated me while slogging through Ice and Fire. To close, I just feel that this story started out great, but has steadily gotten bogged down under the weight of too many characters. This series is becoming reminiscent of \\"The Wheel of Time\\" in the way things just stopped happening. I actually finished the last half of Storm of Swords by reading chapter summaries on a wiki site. I'm glad I did, because I was spared the agony of some later events. I don't know if I'll continue the series or not, because it is just so depressing.

B00AZNEW5G|2.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />Rating: 2.5 out of 5.0<br /><br />Emboldened by her big Oscar win for 'Hurt Locker', Director Kathryn Bigelow was determined to fry some bigger fish, by taking on the herculean task of chronicling the 10 year manhunt for America's most wanted man: Osama bin Laden. Initially, Bigelow partnered with screenwriter Mark Boal, to write about the failed attempt to catch Bin Laden at Tora Bora. Everything changed when bin Laden was caught and disposed of, so Boal scrapped the Tora Bora script and hashed out a new one rather quickly, featuring the now famous mission that resulted in bin Laden's demise.<br /><br />Bigelow's strategy was to create a composite character based on the exploits of numerous CIA agents who were involved in tracking down and eventually uncovering bin Laden's infamous lair. She ended up creating 'Maya' (Jessica Chastain), after hearing about one particular female CIA agent involved in bin Laden's capture. As Rex Reed correctly notes in 'The Observer,' there was no evidence that the hunt for bin Laden was the exclusive province of one woman. In Reed's opinion, Bigelow's desire to create a \\"Wonder Woman\\" character backfires, as it ends up that \\"Ms. Chastain's stoic, textbook approach to the role does nothing to illuminate or enlighten.\\"<br /><br />The bigger problem with 'Zero Dark Thirty' that it's inherently undramatic. Maya is never pitted against a concrete antagonist, who she must butt heads with. In its place are a coterie of both individual terrorists who we get to know for only a short time (Ammar Al-Baluchi, who's water boarded due to his 9/11 connections, or faceless terrorists, such as the ones who attempt to assassinate Maya, when she attempts to drive out of the US Embassy in Pakistan). Bigelow ends up connecting her fictional Maya through sleight of hand: suddenly she just happens to be eating dinner at the Islamabaad Hotel, when it's blown up in 2008 or her good CIA friend Jessica, is killed in the 2009 Camp Chapman attack. It's all good journalism, but Maya's individual drama, feels forced. When Maya does butt heads, it's merely with one of her CIA bosses, who's slow to act on her intelligence, due to orders from above (the new Obama administration certainly is proceeding too slowly for the veteran analyst's tastes).<br /><br />After a series of drawn out CIA torture scenes, as well as the aforementioned historical scenes straight from the CNN breaking news play book, Bigelow's and Boal's journalistic 'research', pays some dividends when we're made privy to the way in which the CIA (Maya) tracks down bin Laden's courier, Abu Ahmed, who eventually leads them to bin Laden himself. Perhaps the most interesting scene in the film, is the way in which Ahmed is tracked down while driving his SUV (he's located through his cell phone signals).<br /><br />While I would argue Bigelow is way overrated in the script department, she does know how to use a camera. The final scene, where the crack team of Navy Seals invades bin Laden's compound, and finishes him off, is expertly choreographed and actually somewhat nail biting (even though everyone knows the outcome).<br /><br />Still, I'll argue Ms. Bigelow is a vastly overrated director, another Hollywood 'flavor of the month' (usually 'flavors of the month', can last a good number of years, before they lose their taste). It's understandable, given the fact that there have been few female directors singled out as significant by the Hollywood powers that be, that suddenly Bigelow finds herself in the spotlight. In Zero Dark Thirty, Bigelow and Boal mistakenly assumed that by merely clipping a few newspaper articles and conducting a few interviews with people connected to the CIA and then inserting a composite character into the action, that's enough to create a compelling story. Their 'mistake' still paid dividends: the overwhelming body of critics as well as a liberal general public, overjoyed by the liberal Obama administration 'win', rubber stamped an ordinary film, as a masterpiece!ma administration 'win', rubber stamped an ordinary film, as a masterpiece!

B009K0OPQQ|3.0|I've been an ardent fan of the show from the beginning, of the extraordinary cast in particular (they get 5 Stars!)- in seasons one and two I waited each week with baited breath for the next episode. But even though I want to see what happens next, and I'm watching season three, some things have troubled me about the show from the start. I've thought about it quite a bit, and though I'm not sure how well I can express it, I'll try. [NOTE: There's an update at the end of my review.]<br /><br />I read recently that the director Sidney Lumet said \\"In a well-written drama, the story comes out of the characters. The characters in a well-written melodrama come out of the story.\\" Though I'm still puzzling over what this means, exactly, by that definition I'd say Once Upon a Time is a melodrama, in that it is plot-driven, rather than character-driven. I think in the best works, in any genre, a great story zigzags back and forth between drama and melodrama, i.e., between plot and character development, each seamlessly informing and enriching the other. ONCE is woefully inadequate in the character development department. The characters are one-dimensional. These fantastic actors (and we fans) deserve so much more.<br /><br />Before you say \\"They're fairy tale characters, for goodness sake, what do you expect?\\" I expect good drama- character development to rival, complement mythic themes. In thinking about the show, it finally dawned on me that the character neglect reminds me of the cognitive deficits in certain syndromes. In autism, for example, especially Asperger's Syndrome, one thing that characterizes many patients is a preoccupation with the trees to the exclusion of the forest. Such patients focus for hours on separate parts, on the mechanics of things. An analogy might be someone intent on cataloging the stars in the sky, one after another, but being unable to appreciate the majesty of the cosmos, feel a sense of awe and wonder when appraising the night sky in its entirety.<br /><br />Similarly, in Oliver Sacks's wonderful book THE MAN WHO MISTOOK HIS WIFE FOR A HAT, he describes the behavior and bizarre \\"reality\\" of patients who've suffered right-hemisphere brain damage. What remains is the left hemisphere, and the way it processes info is by parts and feature analysis. Lacking the right brain, which puts everything together, sees relations, wholes, and attaches feelings and significance to perceptions and events, the left brain focuses on sequential details, linear analysis, which results in a very odd and unsatisfying experience for those affected, sadly. Indeed, patients with Asperger's and RH damage tend to have great difficulties with personal relationships, their perceptual blinders rendering them unable to apprehend other people in all their complexity. People who love them often find interactions extremely frustrating.<br /><br />ONCE reminds me of that brand of reality, a focus on the machinations of the plot, with scant appreciation of the depth, profundity and emotional complexity of human beings, both individually and in concert with each other. The characters' major function is to drive the plot forward, it's the plot itself that take precedence, like a big math problem (albeit at times an intriguing one).<br /><br />The antithesis of this, for me, might be the pithy, splendid TV Series THE CROW, STAIRWAY TO HEAVEN or the movie FAIRY TALE: A TRUE STORY. They succeed as both superb melodrama and superb drama, emotionally affecting, weaving intricate plot lines and characters into tapestries that become far more than the sum of their parts.<br /><br />And another observation, at the risk of sounding sexist- I can't help but wonder if the fact that all the writers on the show are male doesn't contribute to this preoccupation with mechanics and linear details (research has shown that left brain processes tend to predominate in males, whereas females tend to use both hemispheres in tandem). Seriously, if I didn't know better I'd swear the show was written by a bunch of 14 year-old boys with high-level Asperger's Syndrome (some Asperger's folks have high IQs, see the world in simplistic, black and white terms, but construct super-elaborate scenarios around simplified themes from which they're loathe to deviate).<br /><br />[Update: I stand corrected- as others kindly pointed out, they've corrected that situation and have hired female writers, I'm happy to learn.] Fairy tales have traditionally been the province of girls, much more so than boys. Granted, most fairy tales were recorded &/or collected by men, but I've always pictured them being offered as gifts, infused with hopes and dreams, by mothers down through the ages, told as bedtime stories, a feminine oral tradition fueling their children's- especially their daughters'- imaginations. I DO applaud the writers for depicting strong, heroic women, kudos for that. All I know is that what l most love about fairy tales...isn't here, it's missing.<br /><br />Were I the head writer on the show, I'd ensure that the writers focus less on the dizzying intricacies and pyrotechnics of the plot (could it BE more pointlessly complicated??), and slow down a bit, putting flesh on characters' bones and exploring relationships between them. Can you do that without sacrificing clever plot twists, and while still showing what's happening with multiple characters? Sure, ST. ELSEWHERE, for example, did it spectacularly well (the best TV show ever, I think)- and the hit show BORGEN too, from Denmark. And actually, come to think of it, another offering starring Ginnifer Goodwin, HE'S JUST NOT THAT INTO YOU, also succeeded in that respect (at least I think so, not everyone liked it as much as I). In a review of it it was noted that even a brief scene in which Bradley Cooper declined Ben Affleck's offer of a cigarette bespoke volumes about the characters (while also advancing the plot), no wasted moments. Anyway, the marvelous actors on ONCE deserve such character development and subtlety, that's what I'm waiting for, yearning for, anyway...<br /><br />Then again, when I'm feeling a little less magnanimous, I wonder if maybe the writers and producers don't know EXACTLY what they're doing. Now and then there's this nagging feeling that somehow I'm being made a fool of, that maybe someone's having a good laugh at my expense. Why would ANYONE stick around, year after year, given so few crumbs, on a mere promise of something that never seems to materialize? Where's the payoff for my attentiveness, my loyalty? Perhaps some suit said: \\"Mix up the metaphors! Throw in everything but the kitchen sink, THAT'LL keep 'em guessing. Hell, we can even call it a new GENRE!\\" Or: \\"Just toss around a few buzzwords, you know, true love, true hearts, true WHATEVER...human imagination being what it is, they'll supply their OWN profundity. HEY! How about SAVIOR- it's LOADED with associations, throw THAT in!\\" Could there be a standing bet as to how long the show can be drawn out, how much profit can be amassed, how much convoluted but simplistic DRIVEL can be heaped on hapless fans before they begin to get wise? You know the old saying, you can fool some of the people... I haven't given up on ONCE yet, but I'm getting awfully close.<br /><br />UPDATE: The show continued in much the same vein, until Episode 4, Quite a Common Fairy- smashing, brilliant, exactly the kind of stories, with the interpersonal punch, I've longed for. If the show continues with this kind of writing I'll be one happy fan. WELL DONE!<br /><br />[[ASIN:B000OCY7OO The Crow: Stairway To Heaven - The Complete Series]]<br /><br />[[ASIN:B000N8NFJC Fairytale: A True Story]]<br /><br />[[ASIN:0684853949 The Man Who Mistook His Wife For A Hat: And Other Clinical Tales]]<br /><br />[[ASIN:0767010019 The Very Best of St. Elsewhere - Four Tape Set [VHS]]]<br /><br />[[ASIN:B00BELOF14 Borgen - Season 1]]<br /><br />[[ASIN:B002BBJ52U He's Just Not That Into You]]etter I'd swear the show was written by a bunch of 14 year-old boys with high-level Asperger's Syndrome (some Asperger's folks have high IQs, see the world in simplistic, black and white terms, but construct super-elaborate scenarios around simplified themes from which they're loathe to deviate).<br /><br />[Update: I stand corrected- as others kindly pointed out, they've corrected that situation and have hired female writers, I'm happy to learn.] Fairy tales have traditionally been the province of girls, much more so than boys. I DO applaud the writers for depicting strong, heroic women, kudos for that. All I know is that what l most love about fairy tales...isn't here, it's missing.<br /><br />Were I the head writer on the show, I'd ensure that the writers focus less on the dizzying intricacies and pyrotechnics of the plot (could it BE more pointlessly complicated??), and slow down a bit, putting flesh on characters' bones and exploring relationships between them. Can you do that without sacrificing clever plot twists, and while still showing what's happening with multiple characters? Sure, ST. ELSEWHERE, for example, did it spectacularly well (the best TV show ever, I think)- and the hit show BORGEN too, from Denmark. And actually, come to think of it, another offering starring Ginnifer Goodwin, HE'S JUST NOT THAT INTO YOU, also succeeded in that respect (at least I think so, not everyone liked it as much as I). In a review of it it was noted that even a brief scene in which Bradley Cooper declined Ben Affleck's offer of a cigarette bespoke volumes about the characters (while also advancing the plot), no wasted moments. Anyway, the marvelous actors on ONCE deserve such character development and subtlety, that's what I'm waiting for, yearning for, anyway...<br /><br />Then again, when I'm feeling a little less magnanimous, I wonder if maybe the writers and producers don't know EXACTLY what they're doing. Now and then there's this nagging feeling that somehow I'm being made a fool of, that maybe someone's having a good laugh at my expense. Why would ANYONE stick around, year after year, given so few crumbs, on a mere promise of something that never seems to materialize? Where's the payoff for my attentiveness, my loyalty? Perhaps some suit said: \\"Mix up the metaphors! Throw in everything but the kitchen sink, THAT'LL keep 'em guessing. Hell, we can even call it a new GENRE!\\" Or: \\"Just toss around a few buzzwords, you know, true love, true hearts, true WHATEVER...human imagination being what it is, they'll supply their OWN profundity. HEY! How about SAVIOR- it's LOADED with associations, throw THAT in!\\" Could there be a standing bet as to how long the show can be drawn out, how much profit can be amassed, how much convoluted but simplistic DRIVEL can be heaped on hapless fans before they begin to get wise? You know the old saying, you can fool some of the people... I haven't given up on ONCE yet, but I'm getting awfully close.<br /><br />UPDATE: The show continued in much the same vein, until Episode 4, Quite a Common Fairy- smashing, brilliant, exactly the kind of stories, with the interpersonal punch, I've longed for. If the show continues with this kind of writing I'll be one happy fan. B000GTCZWQ|4.0|Whale rider is a heart-wrenching story of a girl, Pai, who believes that she was born to be the next leader of her tribe.  The story takes place in eastern New Zealand among the Whangara people.  They believe that their ancestor, Paikea, rode to shore on the back of a whale when he encountered trouble at sea.  This is a patriarchal tribe that believes the first-born son is a direct descendant of Paikea and that they are destined to be the chiefs of the tribe.  When Pai was born there was a twin brother who died along with her mother.  Her brother was destined to be the next chief.  Pai lives with her grandmother and grandfather, Koro.  They have a special bond and love each other dearly.  Koro is very traditional and will not allow Pai to learn the ways of a Whangara chief.  Instead he gathers all of the first-born males to teach them and then test them to see who will be the next chief.  When he finds out that Pai has also learned all the boys are learning he gets very angry and will not speak to her or rise from his bed.  One night whales start beaching on the sand in their village.  The villagers try to make the whales comfortable and to coax them back into the sea.  There is one whale apart from the others, the biggest whale.  The villagers believe that this is Paikea's whale and they know that if he goes back into the water the other whales will follow.  The villagers cannot get the whale to go back into the water.  After the start leaving the beach to rest, Pai goes over to the whale and the whale goes back into the water with Pai on his back. Pai rides the whale into the sea and is found and rescued a day or so later.  All of the other whales follow.  Koro realizes that Pai is gone and at that moment accepts that she should be the new leader of the tribe.<br /><br /> This movie is very reflective of the indigenous tribes we have studied in our World Religions class.  The tribe has the story of how they came to be through the story of their ancestor Paikea.  They are very strict in their traditions as to the roles of men and women and they have rituals and chants for different ceremonies or occasions.  The indigenous religions we have studied in our class also have their different creation stories and their rites of passage for certain stages of life.  They have songs or chants for all reasons, be it death, birth or a coming of age.  The movie Whale Rider gives the viewer a feeling of how these tribes continue to keep their traditions even in a more modern age.<br /><br />Barnett, John (Producer), & Caro, Niki (Writer/Director). She analyzes our shift from a Culture of Character (Introvert) to a Culture of Personality (Extrovert) which began with the success and influence of Dale Carnegie. She also points out that the school environment of group participation as well as brainstorming business meetings do not work well for our ilk. B001BR5NN0|3.0|Although John Hughes' classic teenage film lacks an exciting plot, its exceptional character development and its timeless message make it a worthwhile watch. The film focuses on five high school students, each from a different social group, stuck in an eight hour Saturday detention. There's Bender, the criminal, Claire, the princess, Andy, the jock, Brian, the brain, and Allison, the basket case. They are stuck in a library with only each other for company, so gradually, even though there is initial resistance, they all begin to talk.<br /> The film's best quality isn't that it has an original, exciting plot, because it doesn't. With one setting, a high school, there is a lack of interesting scenery, and the premise of a film based on high school students was popular during the eighties and became overdone. However, the witty and thought-provoking banter between the characters and the development of each one helps to make up for the uninteresting plot. Each character tells their story, offering tidbits about their family and personal lives and they slowly start to develop into much more than the stereotypical nicknames they're given. At the beginning of the film, as soon as Principal Vernon leaves the room after addressing the students, Bender (Judd Nelson), in true troublemaker fashion, begins trying to get a rise out of Andy (Emilio Estevez) and Claire (Molly Ringwald), and succeeds. Claire points this out to Andy, telling him, \\"Just ignore him.\\" Bender replies with, \\"Sweets? You couldn't ignore me if you tried\\", to which she has no response. Following, there is a moment of intense eye contact between Molly Ringwald and Judd Nelson. This scene sets the stage for the continuous sexual tension between Claire and Bender throughout the film. Bender has some of the most memorable lines of the film and delivers them well, dripping with sarcasm. Estevez does an excellent job of playing the tough jock, but occasionally throws in a sensitive moment. He doesn't hesitate tothreaten Bender after he insults the girls, but when everyone else is shunning Allison (Ally Sheedy), he stops and listens to her. Brian (Anthony Michael Hall) is the typical brain, throwing irrelevant academic information into the conversation and quickly shutting his trap if he is challenged. However, Hughes reveals another side to Brian when they all open up to each other, a side most viewers wouldn't expect. Sheedy does an excellent job of playing the school basket case. Her delivery of biting dialogue and her accurate portrayal of quirky, strange habits are enough to make the viewer believe she is a little bit crazy.<br />At some point or another throughout the day, they all open up and reveal very personal secrets about their lives. It's no surprise to the viewer that each of them isn't exactly as they seem on the outside, and they don't all completely fit their character molds. Andy points this out to all of them saying, \\"We're all pretty bizarre. Some of us are just better at hiding it, that's all.\\" Some of their secrets are predictable, yes, but the problems these kids face are realistic and relatable to current teens, even over twenty years after the film was made.<br />The Breakfast Club may lack originality and a sense of excitement, but there's no doubt the film has a timeless message. Everyone has their problems, even those that seem most put together. Hughes is able to keep the audience appeased while delivering this message, and the film has enough interesting moments to keep the audience entertained. Each viewer will have his or her own opinion on the story, but there is no doubt that The Breakfast Club has become an American classic.

044024630X|2.0|***SPOILER ALERT***<br /><br />The good news: Okay plot, some back-story on the protagonist, and the best use of a midnight train since the Pips. Deus ex diesel engine. I found myself sympathizing with some poor conductor, unseen and unnamed, who by the end of the book must be suffering from a really bad case of post traumatic stress disorder. (Read it, you'll understand.) Despite problems of style and substance, the book at least kept me reading.<br /><br />The bad news:<br /><br />1. Reacher is a murderer. During the course of the book he executes three people. (Actually, he kills a fourth, too, but we'll give him self defense on that one.) Reacher is supposed to be a cop, but he conducts himself like a psychopath. I've known a lot of cops who daily dedicate heart and soul to bringing wrongdoers to justice. Summary execution isn't justice. Any cop who decides to simply murder bad people is a monster. Should we admire the economy of Reacher's \\"justice\\"? He's admirable only if you're the sort of fan who admires Walter White in \\"Breaking Bad\\"--as a ruthlessly efficient bad*** narcissistic destroyer who has no compunction about killing. Count me out--I know a villain when I see one.<br /><br />2. For a protagonist who thinks himself a man of few words, Reacher's narration suffers from logorrhea. Leading up to action, he'll endlessly describe the setting when a few apt words would suffice. This man of action can't get to it without blather. In the tradition of writers of tough-guy fiction, the author wants to employ short sentences (sometimes), but his sentences have a bad habit of piling up like the cars of a derailed train. He'll describe something and then add another very short sentence that restates exactly what the preceding sentence said. It's almost as if he's decided the first sentence is too long, so, as a man of brevity, he adds a shorter sentence to summarize. (It's hard, I suppose, to correct a writer this commercially successful, but a good editor with abig box of red pencils could improve the Reacher books. Not gonna happen.)<br /><br />3. Lee Child is a Brit attempting a classic American literary archetype, and in some ways he gets it right: The loner, a man of action but not words, complete in himself and not tied down by social conventions. Child, however, pushes the archetype further than it will go: Carrying only a toothbrush, a little money, and the clothes on his back, Reacher wanders about the country homeless, randomly stumbling into slam-bang adventure. (For a man who walks everywhere and likes to get romantic with beautiful women, wouldn't it be good to carry a stick of Mitchum Power Gel, too?) Reacher evokes characters like John D. MacDonald's Travis McGee (sadly under-appreciated by modern readers), Ross Macdonald's Lew Archer (ditto), Richard Kimble from \\"The Fugitive\\" TV series, and numerous hard-boiled detectives from earlier decades. But the loner archetype only works if he has an ethical code. The hero can't be just a killing machine, and if he does kill, it must be only out of necessity. Such a character should never appoint himself righteous assassin, otherwise he becomes ethically indistinguishable from John Wilkes Booth.<br /><br />4. I'd hoped that this story would tell us why Reacher adopted his peculiar walkabout lifestyle--was it some traumatic event that delivered a staggering blow to his psyche? Unfortunately, the slight explanation offered up comes across as lame and unsatisfactory. Same for the limp resolution of Reacher's association with the military and relationship with the sexually impeccable Sheriff Elizabeth Deveraux. (As the Pips would say, Woo-Woo, Woo-Woo! Trying to not spoil too much.)<br /><br />This was my third Jack Reacher. I'll probably try another after awhile. If the protagonist continues to be wantonly murderous, the present book will be my penultimate.<br /><br />ON FURTHER REVIEW:<br /><br />After I posted this article I gave it some more thought. (I concede thismay be more thought than the book deserves.)<br /><br />I pointed out that Reacher has two troublesome characteristics: He's an extreme unsocialized loner and a near-psychopathic angel-of-revenge. I also said that the ending of the book didn't satisfactorily explain his decision to go straightaway from his rigid and disciplined military career to a life as a drifter. I'd thought  the book would reveal some horribly traumatic event leading to Reacher's walkabout, but no.<br /><br />I've come up with a new theory: Jack Reacher is profoundly mentally ill, a dangerously insane voices-hearing anti-social drifter who keeps on the move to stay one step ahead of the butterfly net and straitjacket. If so, that makes him a completely unreliable narrator, and his descriptions of his very unlikely lifestyle, superior combat skills, and against-the-grain heroism are in fact only delusions of grandeur. Mental illness might also account for a somewhat obsessive and disorganized prose style. It would explain the real reason the Army cut Reacher loose and why he can't hold onto a relationship.<br /><br />Previously I'd thought of Mr. Child as a British admirer of America and its streak of rugged individualism embodied by the tough-guy loner archetype. But is that the case? Or is Jack Reacher a sly satire on something Mr. Child sees as pathological in the American character, the brutal sociopath representing America going it alone, dangerously cut off from the civilizing forces of the rest of the world?<br /><br />Just a somewhat subversive theory. If it's true, the book is both more thought-provoking and less agreeable.

This novel was very different in style but also illustrated Hosseini's remarkable talent for writing interesting novels.<br /><br />This novel was slower moving with myriads of characters interconnecting with each other. They were either relatives, friends or neighbors spreading over two generations or even three in some cases. The novel begins with a folk tale about a div, a giant in Afghan folklore, who steals the youngest son of an impoverished family in a poverty stricken village. When the father sets out to find his son, the div shows him that the child is living happily in a palace, playing with other children. The father then has to decide whether to turn back and leave his son where he is or take him back to a difficult life in the village.<br /><br />This story is told to Abdullah and Pari, a day before they take a long and difficult journey from their village to Kabul, where the father leaves Pari, the sister of Abdullah, with a rich childless couple as it was difficult for the father to support both children. Abdullah was devastated by this for a long time as he and his sister were very close. The separation of the two siblings forms the basis of this incredible novel which at times seems rather long-winded as it is composed of a number of short stories, told from a number of different angles by the various characters in the book.<br /><br />The actual story begins in 1952 and meanders along with the various players relating their own experiences and their connections with the various characters in the book.<br /><br />Hosseini's medical knowledge in describing the illnesses and handicaps of some of the characters makes the novel even more realistic. The writer is a qualified physician and this blends very well in adding a lot of realism to the novel itself. The characters actually flip out of the pages making the reader empathize with their misfortunes, desires and the brilliance of this writer's portrayal of them makes the book really hard to lay aside.<br /><br />I would recommend that the reader not put the book aside but read it from beginning to end in as short a time as possible. If this is not possible the reader can lose the thread as there are so many characters involved who have much to say from their perspective that one can get confused.<br /><br />This is the reason why I feel that this novel is short of a 5-star rating. Despite this all the loose ends seem to tie up towards the end as the families had spread out over a number of countries. 1451626096|5.0|This review can also be found on my blog at: armywife2310.wordpress.com/2013/09/09/the-white-princess-by-philippa-gregory/<br /><br />Rating:  5 stars<br />Pub date:  23 July 2013<br />Publisher: Touchstone<br />Genre: Adult Fiction, Historical Fiction<br />Format: hardback, obtained from the Public Library<br /><br />Summary:<br /><br />The White Princess opens as the news of the battle of Bosworth is brought to Princess Elizabeth of York, who will learn not only which rival royal house has triumphed, Tudor or York, but also which suitor she must marry: Richard III her lover, or Henry Tudor her enemy.<br /><br />A princess from birth, Elizabeth fell in love with Richard III, though her mother made an arranged betrothal for her with the pretender to the throne: Henry Tudor. When Henry defeats Richard against all odds, Elizabeth has to marry the man who murdered her lover in battle, and create a new royal family with him and his ambitious mother: Margaret Beaufort, The Red Queen. But, while the new monarchy can win, it cannot, it seems, hold power in an England which remembers the House of York with love.<br /><br />The new king's greatest fear is that somewhere, outside England, a prince from the House of York is waiting to invade and re-claim the throne for the house of York. Fearing that none of his new allies can be trusted, Henry turns to his wife to advise him, all the time knowing that her loyalties must be divided. When the young man who would be king finally leads his army and invades England, it is for Elizabeth to decide whether she recognizes him as her brother and a claimant to the throne, or denies him in favor of the husband she is coming to love...<br /><br />As much of a Tudor lover as I am, I must admit that I didn't really know all that much about Henry VII. I knew that his marriage to Elizabeth of York basically ended the Cousins' War (War of the Roses) by merging the two houses (York and Lancaster), but that was about it other than he was the father of Henry VIII.<br /><br />We start just after the battle of Bosworth where Henry Tudor wins over Richard III. Richard III is killed, the crown is stripped from his corpse, and his body is left in shame to be buried in an unknown location. (If you didn't know, he body was recently found and identified within the past year. Cool stuff!) Elizabeth knew either outcome of the battle she would be the next Queen of England. She was Richard III's lover and his wife, Anne Neville, had recently died. She was betrothed to Henry Tudor to secure his supporters in trying to rescue her brothers in the Tower, but when that failed, the betrothal remained anyway and shifted part of the York support to him against Richard.<br /><br />In his way of revenge for her making him look like a fool while she was Richard's mistress, he forces himself on her over and over until she conceives. Only then does he consent to marry her. It is announced that the baby is a \\"honeymoon baby\\" conceived in love and bliss of the beginning of their marriage. She is safely delivered of a son, named Arthur, who is to become Prince of Wales.<br /><br />The royal family is plagued by \\"pretenders\\" to the throne. Boys claiming to be the Princes in the Tower. The first pretender comes to nothing and is taken into the royal service and slowly rises from kitchen boy to falconer. The Dowager Queen Elizabeth, helped support the uprising and is eventually banished into retirement at Bermondsey Abbey. Henry believes that this will be the end of her meddling. He still stays extremely distrustful of his wife, Elizabeth, always saying she still supports her York relatives.<br /><br />Another rebellion rises surrounding her cousin, Edward, Earl of Warwick, who is the son of George, Duke of Clarence, and his wife Isabel Neville. The young earl is then imprisoned in the Tower and never again gains his freedom. He is portrayed as a simple boy who does not fully understand the trouble of being related to the fallen royal family of York. His sister Maggie is a constant companion to Elizabeth.<br /><br />Through the struggle and haunting of \\"the boy,\\" Elizabeth and Henry fall in love for a while. They get over that they were forced into a loveless marriage to secure alliances. The love is fleeting as the threat of the pretender grows and grows over the years. Henry is nearly driven mad with the chase of this boy who claims to be Richard, Duke of York, the younger brother. Elizabeth never admits that she and her mother slipped Richard out of sanctuary and sent a pauper in his place to the Tower.<br /><br />While dealing with the threat of the pretender, Henry struggles to gain the love of the people of England. He taxes them to the brink and is hot and cold in his clemency. The lords and commoners hate him which drive them to support in secret and in the open, the boy called Richard. Elizabeth finds herself caught in the middle. She doesn't know if she should stand by her husband or turn to the boy who claims he is her brother. She ultimately decides to stand by her husband and her children.<br /><br />After the threat of uprisings in England seem to die down and chasing the pretender all over Europe, Richard lands in England. He is taken prisoner at Exeter and Henry decides to be lenient and keep him as a prisoner. He also falls in love with Richard's wife, Katherine, who is cousin to the Scottish king. He promises her that as long as the pretender does as his parole commands, he will not be harmed.<br /><br />Henry hopes that in showing the pretender to the people and having created a history for him, the people of England will turn against him and finally support Henry. This plan backfires. \\"The boy\\" has all of the charm of the York family and draws more supporters. Henry then keeps him close at court. At the Palace of Sheen, there is a fire in the wardrobe, where \\"the boy\\" is staying. Henry thinks there is no way he could have survived and is delighted in the beginning until he realises he was duped and he had been in his wife's rooms that night and was safe. Elizabeth fears that Henry set the blaze himself in order to be free of the pretender.<br /><br />After a progress, the royal family returns to the Tower. In the middle of the night, \\"the boy\\" flees from his guard. It is believed he drugged the guards to escape. He is quickly found and placed as a prisoner in the rooms that the Princes in the Tower were originally placed in. He is on the floor above his \\"cousin\\" Edward of Warwick. Henry then tries to entrap them in plots. He finally confesses this to Elizabeth as he realises that his plan is backfiring against him and more people than those he ordered to entrap the boys are involved. Finally, he has no choice but to pursue execution.<br /><br />Henry tires of waiting for the court to draw up a jury and has the verdict called out against \\"the boy\\" before he even faces a trial. He is executed by hanging, Henry deciding to be lenient and not have him drawn and quartered as well as hanged. The day he is hanged, Elizabeth hears the singing of Melusina, the founder of her house, which is a signal that one of her household died. Elizabeth is further convinced that besides looking like her brother, Richard, that he was in deed her brother.<br /><br />Days after\\"the boy\\" was executed, Edward of Warwick was found guilty of treason and executed. Elizabeth's half-brother, Thomas Grey, believed that he didn't fully understand what was happening. He was a simply boy and had spent over half of his life in captivity in the Tower.<br /><br />After the executions, Henry withdraws from court claiming sickness. Rumours start swirling around court and Elizabeth goes to confront him. His mother explains that he deeply cared for \\"the boy,\\" because he was the only one who could understand Henry. Not fully understanding, she confronts Henry telling him he must return to his court. He then explains that he is riddled with guilt over executing two innocent boys. Hesays that only \\"the boy\\" understood what Henry had gone through as a child in exile. Growing up being told he was born for greatness and to wait until the time was right to come forward.<br /><br />Elizabeth admits to Henry, after she had admitted to her confessor, that she and her mother had placed a curse on the murderer of the boys. The curse was that whoever harmed the princes would lose their own son and a grandson in childhood and that their line would end with a daughter. Both the confessor and Henry scoff that there is no such thing as curses. Henry goes further to say that he never harmed her brothers and that they are safe in their succession with three boys. Elizabeth points out that her mother also had three sons and died with no heirs but girls. Henry says that he had to kill the boys otherwise they never would've been safe on their throne. He pleads for forgiveness and reluctantly Elizabeth grants it, realising there is much she has to forgive.<br /><br />Though this is where the book ends, this is not the end of the story of Elizabeth of York. If you know Tudor history, you could say that by the wording of the curse, either Henry or his mother had Edward killed in the Tower as well as the pauper (if a swap occurred), then if \\"the boy\\" known as Perkin Warbeck truly was Richard of York, the curse played out against them. Arthur Prince of Wales dies in adolescence. His younger brother, Henry, eventually succeeds to the throne, and leaves as his heir, a sickly boy in Edward VI. Edward also dies in adolescence, leaving as his heirs, his older sisters, Mary and Elizabeth. Both die childless, thus ending the male Tudor line.<br /><br />We don't know what really happened with the Princes in the Tower, but to me, this account seems likely. Otherwise, why would Henry VII have spent so much time to create a back story for the boy who was executed with no name, but was also called Perkin Warbeck? Also, if the boy wasn't a true claimant to the throne, why would somany courts of Europe and so many English nobles have supported him? Who knows? It's a great mystery!<br /><br />I can't wait to see how the last book plays into the series and how it ties in with the Tudors series Gregory has also written. I would definitely recommend this book and series! It's been so full of feels! I also enjoyed learning more about the beginnings of a family I've loved studying for years. Seriously, you should see my nonfiction shelf. It is FULL of Elizabeth I books. I've written more reports on her than I care to admit haha.<br /><br />Recommended for: History lovers, those interested in the first Tudor king, Elizabeth of York, and the mystery surrounding the Princes in the Tower<br />Not recommended for: History haters, those who have no interest in English history - especially Tudor history or the Princes in the Tower

B00EV4EUT8|4.0|First things first: although \\"The Butler\\" aka \\"Lee Daniels' The Butler\\" (the longer name forced on the film by a Warner Brothers' copyright threat over their own nearly 100-year-old film \\"The Butler\\") is based to some extent on the true story of Eugene Allen, who did in fact serve under seven presidents and lived to see Barack Obama become the first African-American President, a great many of the details in the film are fictionalized for dramatic effect.  If this is a major issue for you (it is for many folks), be forewarned then; a significant example occurs early on, as our title character (named Cecil Gaines in the film) leaves the plantation he's working on in the south largely because of an incident of violence that did not in fact happen to the real Eugene Allen.  There are other important details in the film that are fictionalized or altered; this one is worth mentioning because it makes for a greater contrast between the life he left and the life he gained than there actually was.  Given the harsh realities of black life before, during, and even after the Civil Rights era, I'm not sure it was necessary to begin the film with such violence.<br /><br />But this is not, for me, a huge problem, and I think the film's general thrust - that a black man's service to the men who occupied the highest office in the land did not exempt him from indignities small and large, and that there was still blatant racism even in the White House, even years after MLK, Malcolm X, and the Civil Rights Act - rings true and is generally well and potently told.  Forest Whitaker, one of the finest American actors of his generation in this reviewer's opinion, gives a typically excellent and well-rounded performance, playing a character who is essentially passive for much of the film but managing to give him plenty of pathos, dignity, and occasional moments of emotional power.  I don't think it's anything close to Whitaker's best performance - that would be his monumental portrayal of Charlie Parker in [[ASIN:B000053V7P Bird]] - but the difficulties in playing a static character who must hide his emotions over much of his life shouldn't be underestimated.  David Oyelowo as Gaines' eldest son Louis - Freedom Rider, close associate of Dr. King, Black Panther, and ultimately Congressman - is just as impressive, and the rocky relationship between the father who is willing (generally) to wait for black lives to get better, and the angry son who will do anything to help create equality NOW, is at the heart of the story.<br /><br />Louis, though, does cast a light on one of the problems with the film - the need to hit every historical event possible, to put the Gaines family in the center of as much of black experience as can possibly be shown from the 60s through the present.  Louis' achievements aren't impossible and are mirrored in some real-life people, but added to all of the close associations that his father seems to have with every President - being alone with Jackie as she weeps over JFK, hearing Reagan defend his South Africa policies but then later listening to him wonder aloud if he was right, handing LBJ his toilet paper, etc - does make it all seem a little \\"Forrest Gump\\" at times.  And the famous names who play the Presidents, including Robin Williams as Ike, John Cusack as Nixon, and Alan Rickman as Reagan, distract as much as they add any dramatic urgency to the story. I'm also not entirely sure that Daniels and his screenwriter Danny Strong needed to soft-pedal the actions and attitudes of the Presidents themselves; the film leaves you with the feeling that the men who served in the office were all (eventually) pretty decent-minded when it came to their attitudes about race (though admitting that LBJ, Nixon and Reagan had quite imperfect records) and one feels that the blame is placed almost entirely on southern racists.  It feels a little gutless at times, in refusing to really take these men or their administrations to task.  As the film draws to an end, it all adds up to a frankly predictable, play-your-heartstrings finale that honestly didn't need to be pitched at the emotional heights that it hit. The real story - or something closer to it - would have felt a bit more satisfying to me.  But if you're a big fan of the traditional Hollywood ending, you may not mind; and I will say that there was a tear or two on my face, despite the \\"oh, of course this is the way he'll end it\\" feeling.<br /><br />And a big part of that lump-in-the-throat effect, I'm pleased to say, was due to Oprah Winfrey's outstanding portrayal of Gloria, Cecil's brash, sexually charged, hard-drinking, but world-weary and always caring wife Gloria.  I'll say right off that I've never been a huge fan of Oprah's TV show, or her public persona.  Maybe it's because she often seems to be putting on an act even when I'm fairly sure she's not; and I think that's because she really is a born performer.  I HAVE been a big fan of her as an actress, and her Gloria is almost as memorable as her Sethe in Jonathan Demme's criminally underrated [[ASIN:B00C4Q0238 Beloved]].  There's a wonderful toughness and brittleness and intelligence in Gloria, but also a crazy side, and one of the major achievements of \\"The Butler\\" is as a convincing portrait of a tumultuous marriage in which each partner really does seem to realistically complement the other.<br /><br />So to sum up, this certainly isn't a film that feels real as history - a little too much is stuffed in - and it's a little too Hollywood and sentimental at the end for my tastes.  But it does feel emotionally true to the periods, and it is well worth seeing for the excellent performances, especially that of Oprah Winfrey.  I don't think the great film about the American Civil Rights era has been made yet, but \\"The Butler\\" does get more right than it gets wrong, and it's eminently watchable.ilm draws to an end, it all adds up to a frankly predictable, play-your-heartstrings finale that honestly didn't need to be pitched at the emotional heights that it hit.
